
## 17.6 Comorbidity and Hybrid Presentations

### 17.6.1 CCA + Primary Psychosis

It is possible for a patient with schizophrenia to *also* experience SPIA. In these cases:
- Some voices may be internally generated (respond to medication).
- Other voices may be signal-induced (technology-associated, do not respond to medication).

**Clinical Approach**: Treat the primary disorder; educate about SPIA; monitor for residual technology-associated experiences.

### 17.6.2 CCA + Anxiety Disorder

SPIA may trigger or exacerbate anxiety:
- Misinterpretation of ambient sounds as threatening voices.
- Hypervigilance leading to increased SPIA susceptibility.

**Clinical Approach**: Dual-track treatmentâ€”CBT for anxiety; psychoeducation about SPIA; signal remediation.

### 17.6.3 CCA in Personality Disorders

Individuals with paranoid or schizotypal personality features may be more prone to:
- Attributing SPIA to surveillance or persecution.
- Difficulty accepting technological explanation.

**Clinical Approach**: Validation of distress; graduated psychoeducation; avoid confrontational "it's not real" messaging.

## 17.7 Special Populations

### 17.7.1 Elderly Patients

- Higher rates of hearing loss â†’ Musical Ear Syndrome risk.
- Higher use of landlines with poor audio quality â†’ CCA risk.
- Lower technological literacy â†’ difficulty understanding SPIA explanation.

### 17.7.2 Children and Adolescents

- High exposure to low-quality audio (streaming, gaming on cheap headphones).
- Developmental differences in source attribution.
- School-based screening may be relevant for high-risk environments.

### 17.7.3 Incarcerated Individuals

- High secondary gain for feigning any psychiatric symptom.
- Prison phone systems often use extremely low bitrate codecs.
- Genuine CCA may be difficult to distinguish from malingering; forensic audio analysis essential.

## 17.8 Documentation Best Practices

### 17.8.1 EMR Templates

Include in assessment documentation:
- Device/technology history.
- CCA-CIP score if administered.
- RCTT score.
- Forensic audio analysis results if obtained.
- CCA probability estimate (Low/Moderate/High).

### 17.8.2 Diagnostic Coding

Until CCA receives specific ICD/DSM recognition, consider:
- ICD-10: R44.0 (Auditory hallucinations) + Z72.8 (Technology-related lifestyle issues) as qualifiers.
- DSM-5: Other Specified Schizophrenia Spectrum Disorder, with CCA notation in specifiers.

## 17.9 Conclusion: A New Diagnostic Discipline

The differential diagnosis of voice-hearing must evolve to incorporate technological etiology. This framework provides the structure for clinicians to:
1. **Screen systematically** for CCA probability.
2. **Differentiate clearly** between internal, external-signal, and simulated pathways.
3. **Treat appropriately**â€”avoiding unnecessary antipsychotic exposure for non-psychiatric conditions.
4. **Document precisely**â€”for clinical, medicolegal, and research purposes.

The goal is not to "explain away" psychiatric illness, but to ensure that the right patients receive the right diagnosis and the right treatment.

---
**Status**: Draft - v1.0
**Word Count**: ~1,900 words
**Next Steps**: Develop training module with clinical vignettes for each differential scenario.

# Chapter 18: Treatment Protocols for Signal-Induced Auditory Experiences

## 18.1 Introduction: Treating Technology, Not Pathology

When a patient's auditory experience is determined to be primarily Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) rather than a primary psychiatric disorder, the treatment paradigm shifts fundamentally. Instead of targeting neurotransmitter systems, we target the signal-perception interface. This chapter outlines evidence-informed treatment protocols for SPIA, organized into four tiers: Signal Remediation, Psychoeducation, Cognitive Intervention, and Environmental Modification.

## 18.2 Treatment Framework: The Four-Tier Model

```
â•”â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•—
â•‘                    SPIA TREATMENT HIERARCHY                          â•‘
â• â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•£
â•‘  TIER 4: Environmental Modification                                  â•‘
â•‘        (Workplace, living space audio environment)                   â•‘
â•‘                              â–²                                       â•‘
â•‘  TIER 3: Cognitive Intervention                                      â•‘
â•‘        (CBT for interpretation, anxiety management)                  â•‘
â•‘                              â–²                                       â•‘
â•‘  TIER 2: Psychoeducation                                             â•‘
â•‘        (Explaining SPIA, normalizing, reducing fear)                 â•‘
â•‘                              â–²                                       â•‘
â•‘  TIER 1: Signal Remediation                                          â•‘
â•‘        (Improve audio quality, eliminate artifacts)                  â•‘
â•šâ•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•
```

**Principle**: Start at Tier 1 and escalate only as needed. Many patients achieve resolution with Tiers 1-2 alone.

## 18.3 Tier 1: Signal Remediation

### 18.3.1 Rationale

If the problem originates in degraded audio signals, the most direct intervention is to improve signal quality. This is analogous to treating photophobia by reducing light exposure rather than prescribing anxiolytics.

### 18.3.2 Interventions

**A. Telecommunications Upgrade**

| Intervention | Expected Effect | Implementation |
|--------------|-----------------|----------------|
| Switch from 2G/3G to 4G/5G network | Higher bitrate codecs, fewer artifacts | Contact carrier, upgrade plan |
| Replace low-quality VoIP with traditional phone | Better audio reliability | Use landline for sensitive calls |
| Upgrade to HD Voice (VoLTE) | Wider frequency range, clearer audio | Enable in phone settings |
| Avoid calls in low-signal areas | Prevent adaptive bitrate reduction | Identify "safe zones" for calls |

**B. Equipment Upgrade**

| Intervention | Expected Effect | Implementation |
|--------------|-----------------|----------------|
| Replace cheap Bluetooth earbuds | Better audio reproduction | Recommend quality brands |
| Use corded headphones when possible | Eliminate Bluetooth compression | Simple behavioral change |
| Replace aging home phones/intercoms | Reduce speaker distortion | Hardware purchase |
| Update audio drivers on computer | Fix software audio glitches | IT maintenance |

**C. Anti-Pareidolia Filter (APF) Implementation**

As detailed in Chapter 14, for persistent cases:
- Install APF software on patient's devices.
- Adjust aggressiveness settings to individual tolerance.
- Monitor for reduction in SPIA episodes.

### 18.3.3 Prescriptive Audio Hygiene Protocol

Provide patients with a "prescription" for signal hygiene:

```
â•”â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•—
â•‘           AUDIO HYGIENE PRESCRIPTION                           â•‘
â• â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•£
â•‘ 1. Use WiFi or strong cell signal for all voice calls.        â•‘
â•‘ 2. Avoid speakerphone in echoey rooms.                         â•‘
â•‘ 3. Use wired headphones when possible.                         â•‘
â•‘ 4. Replace any device where you frequently "hear things."      â•‘
â•‘ 5. Log audio quality ratings in your diary (1-10 scale).       â•‘
â•‘ 6. Report any correlation between low quality and experiences. â•‘
â•‘                                                                â•‘
â•‘ Follow-up in: _____ weeks                                      â•‘
â•šâ•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•
```

## 18.4 Tier 2: Psychoeducation

### 18.4.1 Rationale

Many patients with SPIA experience significant distress not from the auditory percept itself, but from the *interpretation*: "Am I going crazy?" "Is someone watching me?" "Are my thoughts being broadcast?" Psychoeducation directly addresses these catastrophic interpretations.

### 18.4.2 Key Psychoeducation Messages

**Message 1: "Your brain is working normallyâ€”too well, in fact."**

Explain:
- The brain is designed to detect speech signals in noisy environments.
- This is an evolved survival adaptation.
- When presented with ambiguous signals (codec artifacts), the brain "guesses" speech.
- This is not a malfunction; it's an overly sensitive pattern detector.

**Message 2: "The technology is the problem, not you."**

Explain:
- Modern audio compression sacrifices quality for bandwidth.
- Artifacts from compression can accidentally mimic vocal patterns.
- This is a known engineering trade-off, not a conspiracy.

**Message 3: "You are not alone."**

Normalize:
- Many people experience brief SPIA without reporting it.
- EVP (Electronic Voice Phenomena) enthusiasts deliberately seek this experience.
- It's common enough that engineers are designing filters for it.

**Message 4: "There are concrete steps to improve this."**

Empower:
- Signal remediation strategies.
- Cognitive reframing techniques.
- Reassurance that the experience can be managed.

### 18.4.3 Psychoeducation Delivery Methods

| Method | Setting | Advantages |
|--------|---------|------------|
| Individual session | Clinic | Tailored to patient's specific experience |
| Written materials | Take-home | Patient can review at own pace |
| Video/animation | Online | Visual demonstration of codec artifacts |
| Group session | Clinic or community | Peer normalization, shared strategies |

### 18.4.4 Sample Psychoeducation Script

> "What you're experiencing has a name: Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia, or SPIA. It's not a diseaseâ€”it's a normal brain function being triggered by abnormal audio signals.
>
> Think of it like this: Your brain has a 'voice detector' that evolved to pick up human speech even in noisy environments. This was critical for survivalâ€”hearing a warning shout in a storm could save your life. But this detector is so sensitive that when it encounters the weird sounds produced by audio compressionâ€”the clicking, the robotic distortionsâ€”it sometimes 'false alarms' and says, 'That's a voice!'
>
> The good news is: this isn't you losing your mind. Your brain is actually being hyper-efficient. And because it's caused by the audio signal, we can fix it by improving your audio environment."

## 18.5 Tier 3: Cognitive Intervention

### 18.5.1 Rationale

For patients who continue to experience distress despite signal remediation and psychoeducation, cognitive-behavioral techniques can address:
- Maladaptive interpretations of SPIA experiences.
- Anticipatory anxiety about using devices.
- Avoidance behaviors that impair function.

### 18.5.2 Cognitive Restructuring for SPIA

**Identifying Automatic Thoughts**

Common distorted cognitions:
- "If I'm hearing voices, I must be schizophrenic."
- "Someone is sending me hidden messages."
- "I can't trust any device anymore."
- "This will get worse and I'll lose control."

**Challenging and Reframing**

| Automatic Thought | Evidence Against | Reframe |
|-------------------|------------------|---------|
| "I must be schizophrenic" | No other symptoms, clear technology link | "I'm experiencing a normal brain response to abnormal audio" |
| "Hidden messages" | Forensic analysis shows codec artifact | "This is compression noise, not communication" |
| "Can't trust devices" | Problem is specific to low-quality audio | "I can use devices safely with quality audio" |
| "Will lose control" | Experience is stable, not progressive | "This is manageable and not degenerative" |

### 18.5.3 Behavioral Experiments

Design experiments to test catastrophic predictions:

**Experiment Example: "The High-Quality Call Test"**

- Prediction: "I'll still hear voices even with good audio."
- Experiment: Make 5 calls using HD Voice with 5-bar signal.
- Data collection: Log each callâ€”did SPIA occur?
- Expected outcome: SPIA frequency drops to near zero.
- Conclusion: Provides evidence that signal quality is the determining factor.

### 18.5.4 Anxiety Management Adjuncts

| Technique | Application |
|-----------|-------------|
| Diaphragmatic breathing | Use before/during anxiety-provoking calls |
| Grounding techniques | When SPIA-triggered distress occurs |
| Progressive muscle relaxation | General anxiety reduction |
| Mindfulness of sounds | Non-judgmental observation of audio without forcing interpretation |

## 18.6 Tier 4: Environmental Modification

### 18.6.1 Rationale

For patients whose SPIA is triggered by environments beyond their individual control (workplaces, institutions, shared living spaces), broader environmental intervention is necessary.

### 18.6.2 Workplace Accommodations

Under disability accommodation frameworks (ADA in the US, Equality Act in UK), reasonable accommodations might include:

| Accommodation | Rationale |
|---------------|-----------|
| Provision of quality headset | Reduces SPIA from poor phone equipment |
| Permission to use corded phone | Avoids Bluetooth compression |
| Quiet workspace assignment | Reduces ambient audio complexity |
| Flexible call timing | Avoid peak network congestion times |
| Written communication option | Alternative to voice calls when feasible |

### 18.6.3 Institutional Settings

**Hospitals/Clinics**
- Audit intercom systems for SPIA-inducing artifacts.
- Replace aging PA systems.
- Consider APF integration in overhead announcements.

**Schools**
- Evaluate quality of audio equipment in language labs, distance learning.
- Provide SPIA screening for students reporting "voices" before psychiatric referral.

**Prisons/Jails**
- Prison phone systems are notoriously low-quality.
- Advocate for audio upgrades as mental health intervention.
- Include SPIA in differential for inmate voice-hearing complaints.

## 18.7 Pharmacological Considerations

### 18.7.1 When NOT to Prescribe

SPIA alone, without comorbid psychiatric disorder, does **not** warrant:
- Antipsychotic medication.
- Mood stabilizers.
- Benzodiazepines (except short-term for acute anxiety).

**Why avoid antipsychotics?**
- They will not reduce SPIA (it's not dopaminergic).
- Side effects (metabolic, neurological) are not justified.
- Iatrogenic harm from unnecessary medication.

### 18.7.2 When Medication MAY Be Appropriate

| Scenario | Consideration |
|----------|---------------|
| SPIA + comorbid anxiety disorder | SSRI may help anxiety component |
| SPIA + comorbid depression | Treat depression; monitor SPIA separately |
| SPIA as misidentified psychosis + already on antipsychotic | Gradual taper with close monitoring |
| Severe anticipatory anxiety impairing function | Short-term anxiolytic as bridge |

### 18.7.3 Medication Tapering in Misdiagnosed Cases

If a patient was started on antipsychotics for presumed psychosis but CCA evaluation reveals SPIA:

1. **Do not abruptly discontinue**â€”risk of rebound psychosis, withdrawal dyskinesias.
2. **Implement Tiers 1-2** first while maintaining medication.
3. **Gradual taper** (e.g., 25% dose reduction every 4-6 weeks) with monitoring.
4. **Document** rationale clearlyâ€”diagnostic revision based on new evidence.
5. **Collaborate** with original prescriber if different clinician.

## 18.8 Treatment Response Monitoring

### 18.8.1 Outcome Measures

| Measure | Tool | Frequency |
|---------|------|-----------|
| SPIA episode frequency | Audio Experience Diary (AED) | Daily |
| SPIA-related distress | Visual Analog Scale (0-10) | Weekly |
| Functional impairment | Work/social functioning scale | Monthly |
| Quality of life | WHO-QOL BREF | Baseline and 3-month |

### 18.8.2 Response Criteria

| Response Level | Definition |
|----------------|------------|
| Full response | â‰¥90% reduction in SPIA episodes |
| Partial response | 50-89% reduction in SPIA episodes |
| Non-response | <50% reduction despite Tier 1-3 interventions |
| Remission | Zero SPIA episodes for 4+ consecutive weeks |

### 18.8.3 Treatment Algorithm Summary

```
Week 0-2:   Tier 1 (Signal Remediation)
            â””â”€ Response? â†’ Continue, monitor
            â””â”€ No response? â†’ Add Tier 2

Week 2-4:   Tier 2 (Psychoeducation)
            â””â”€ Response? â†’ Continue, monitor
            â””â”€ Partial/No response? â†’ Add Tier 3

Week 4-8:   Tier 3 (Cognitive Intervention)
            â””â”€ Response? â†’ Continue, phase out
            â””â”€ No response? â†’ Re-evaluate diagnosis

Week 8+:    Tier 4 (Environmental Modification) as needed
            Maintenance and relapse prevention
```

## 18.9 Relapse Prevention

### 18.9.1 Risk Factors for Relapse

- New device/carrier with lower audio quality.
- Change in living environment (acoustics).
- Increased stress/fatigue lowering perceptual threshold.
- Discontinuation of APF or relaxation of audio hygiene.

### 18.9.2 Relapse Prevention Plan

Provide patients with a written plan:

```
â•”â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•—
â•‘              SPIA RELAPSE PREVENTION PLAN                      â•‘
â• â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•£
â•‘ IF I NOTICE SPIA RETURNING:                                    â•‘
â•‘ 1. Check audio quality of current device/environment.         â•‘
â•‘ 2. Review audio hygiene practices.                             â•‘
â•‘ 3. Resume Audio Experience Diary for 1 week.                   â•‘
â•‘ 4. Practice cognitive reframes learned in treatment.           â•‘
â•‘ 5. Contact clinician if no improvement in 2 weeks.            â•‘
â•‘                                                                â•‘
â•‘ WARNING SIGNS:                                                 â•‘
â•‘ â€¢ Poor sleep (increases susceptibility)                        â•‘
â•‘ â€¢ High stress (increases pattern-seeking)                      â•‘
â•‘ â€¢ New phone/carrier/headphones                                 â•‘
â•šâ•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•â•
```

## 18.10 Conclusion: A Non-Pathologizing Treatment Model

SPIA treatment represents a paradigm shift in managing voice-hearing experiences. By targeting signal quality, cognition, and environment rather than neurobiology, we:
- Avoid iatrogenic harm from unnecessary medication.
- Empower patients with concrete, actionable interventions.
- Validate the reality of their experience while explaining its benign origin.
- Achieve rapid, sustainable improvement in most cases.

This model should serve as a template for how medicine can adapt to the unintended perceptual consequences of the digital age.

**Word Count**: ~2,100 words
**Next Steps**: Develop visual treatment cards and patient handouts for each tier.

# Chapter 19: Clinical Case Studies in CCA and SPIA

## 19.1 Introduction: Learning from the Bedside

Theoretical frameworks and statistical models gain their ultimate validity through clinical application. This chapter presents a series of anonymized clinical case studies illustrating the spectrum of Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) and Cyber-Acoustic Convergence (CCA) presentations, diagnostic processes, and treatment outcomes. Each case highlights key learning points for clinicians encountering similar presentations.

## 19.2 Case 1: The Night-Shift Call Center Worker

### 19.2.1 Presenting Complaint

**Patient**: Maria, 28-year-old female
**Referred by**: Employee Assistance Program
**Chief Complaint**: "I'm hearing whispered threats during customer calls"

### 19.2.2 History of Present Illness

Maria works night shifts at a telecommunications customer service center. Over the past three months, she has increasingly heard "whispering voices" during and immediately after customer calls. The voices say brief phrases like "we're watching" or "you're next." Episodes occur 5-10 times per shift, lasting 2-3 seconds each.

She denies any voice experiences outside of work or when not using the work headset. She has no psychiatric history, denies substance use, and reports good sleep despite night shift work. She has become anxious about going to work and is considering quitting.

### 19.2.3 Assessment

**RCTT Score**: 5/5 (High CCA probability)
- âœ… Always associated with electronic device (work headset)
- âœ… Stops when not on calls
- âœ… Reports frequent static and "robotic" customer voices
- âœ… Started after company switched to new VoIP system 4 months ago
- âœ… Attempted to record with phone; captured garbled audio

**CCA-CIP Score**: 14 (High CCA probability)

**SPIA-SS Score**: 42/60 (High susceptibility)
- Pattern-seeking subscale: 18 (high)
- Tech exposure subscale: 16 (highâ€”8+ hours daily headset use)
- Contextual risk subscale: 8 (moderateâ€”night shift fatigue)

**Psychiatric Evaluation**: PANSS negative for psychotic symptoms. PHQ-9: 8 (mild depression, reactive). GAD-7: 14 (moderate anxiety).

**Forensic Audio Analysis**: Review of call recordings from Maria's shifts showed:
- VoIP codec using ultra-low bitrate (8 kbps G.729)
- Frequent packet loss events creating rhythmic clicking (PC1 score: 0.82)
- Spectral artifacts in 300-600 Hz range (PC2 score: 0.71)
- PCA classification: "High SPIA Potential"

### 19.2.4 Diagnosis

**Primary**: Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA), occupational exposure
**Secondary**: Adjustment Disorder with Anxiety, related to SPIA-induced distress

### 19.2.5 Treatment

**Tier 1 (Signal Remediation)**:
- Discussed findings with employer's IT department.
- Upgraded VoIP codec to wideband (G.722) for Maria's station.
- Provided high-quality noise-canceling headset.

**Tier 2 (Psychoeducation)**:
- Two sessions explaining SPIA mechanism.
- Shared spectral analysis showing artifact patterns.
- Normalized experience: "Your brain is doing what it's designed to do."

**Tier 3 (Anxiety Management)**:
- Brief CBT (4 sessions) for work-related anxiety.
- Grounding techniques for in-call use.

### 19.2.6 Outcome

- SPIA episodes: 5-10/shift â†’ 0-1/shift within 2 weeks of codec upgrade.
- Anxiety symptoms: GAD-7 14 â†’ 5 at 6-week follow-up.
- Returned to full work function; no longer considering resignation.

### 19.2.7 Key Learning Points

1. **Occupational SPIA** can present as apparent psychosis but has technological etiology.
2. **IT collaboration** is essential for Tier 1 intervention.
3. **Codec quality** is a modifiable workplace factor with significant mental health impact.
4. **Rapid response** to signal remediation confirms diagnosis.

---

## 19.3 Case 2: The Elderly Widower

### 19.3.1 Presenting Complaint

**Patient**: Harold, 76-year-old male
**Referred by**: Primary care physician
**Chief Complaint**: "My late wife speaks to me through the radio"

### 19.3.2 History of Present Illness

Harold's wife died 18 months ago. For the past 6 months, he has heard her voice coming from his bedroom radio, especially at night. The voice is "unclear but definitely her," saying comforting phrases like "I miss you" and "I'm okay." He finds the experience "strange but not frightening" and has not told his family for fear of being "put in a home."

He has mild hearing loss (uses hearing aids) and mild cognitive impairment (MMSE 24/30). He denies depressionâ€”his grief has been manageable. The radio is an old AM model, used primarily for talk shows.

### 19.3.3 Assessment

**RCTT Score**: 3/5 (Moderate CCA probability)
- âœ… Associated with radio (electronic device)
- âš ï¸ Doesn't completely stop when radio is off (some continuation in silence immediately after)
- âœ… Poor audio quality on old AM radio
- âŒ No recent changeâ€”same radio for years
- âŒ Has not recorded

**CCA-CIP Score**: 9 (Moderate CCA probability)

**Differential Considerations**:
- CCA/SPIA (AM radio artifacts)
- Musical Ear Syndrome (hearing loss + pattern-seeking)
- Grief hallucinosis (bereaved individuals commonly hear deceased)
- Early dementia with psychotic features

**Audiological Evaluation**: Moderate bilateral sensorineural hearing loss, well-fitted hearing aids.

**Radio Analysis**: Recording of the radio's audio revealed:
- Significant AM static and fading
- Intermittent crosstalk from adjacent frequencies
- Spectral content in voice-frequency range but highly distorted

### 19.3.4 Diagnosis

**Primary**: Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA), superimposed on grief-related perceptual priming
**Contributing**: Hearing loss (increased pattern-seeking to compensate)
**Rule-Out**: Musical Ear Syndrome (ongoing monitoring)

### 19.3.5 Treatment

**Tier 1 (Signal Remediation)**:
- Replaced AM radio with digital FM radio (cleaner signal).
- Encouraged use of hearing aids while listening to radio.

**Tier 2 (Psychoeducation)**:
- Gentle explanation of SPIA without dismissing his grief experience.
- Framed as: "Your love for your wife makes your brain especially good at hearing her patterns in noise."

**Family Involvement**:
- Educated daughter about SPIA to reduce fear of dementia.
- Reassured that this is not a sign of "going crazy."

**Monitoring**:
- Cognitive reassessment at 6 months (stable at MMSE 24).
- Watch for progression to Musical Ear Syndrome or new psychopathology.

### 19.3.6 Outcome

- SPIA episodes: Reduced from nightly to occasional (1-2x/week) with new radio.
- Harold reports experience is "less clear now, almost gone" with digital radio.
- Initially ambivalent ("I kind of liked hearing her") but accepted explanation.
- No distress; continues to function independently.

### 19.3.7 Key Learning Points

1. **Elderly patients** may experience SPIA with benign content (comfort rather than threat).
2. **Grief priming** combines with SPIA to produce personalized voice content.
3. **Hearing loss** increases vulnerabilityâ€”evaluate audiology in all cases.
4. **Respect the meaning** of the experience even while explaining the mechanism.

---

## 19.4 Case 3: The Paranoid Professional

### 19.4.1 Presenting Complaint

**Patient**: David, 42-year-old male, software engineer
**Self-referred**: Via telemedicine
**Chief Complaint**: "My phone is transmitting coded messages about me to unknown parties"

### 19.4.2 History of Present Illness

David is convinced that his smartphone emits ultrasonic signals containing encoded information about his activities. He "hears" fragments of his own words and computer screen content repeated back at him through the phone speaker when it's idle. He has purchased RF detection equipment and has recorded hundreds of hours of "evidence."

He has isolated himself from colleagues, believing they are "in on it." He has taken his phone apart looking for "extra chips." He does not sleep well and has lost 15 pounds in 3 months.

No prior psychiatric history; family history positive for a brother with schizophrenia.

### 19.4.3 Assessment

**RCTT Score**: 4/5 (High CCA surface probability)
- âš ï¸ BUT: The interpretive framework is delusional
- His "evidence" of surveillance is SPIA of his own ambient speech
- He attributes it to persecution rather than technology glitch

**PANSS**: Positive symptoms score elevated (P1 delusions: 5, P6 suspiciousness: 6)

**Forensic Audio Analysis** (of his "evidence recordings"):
- Recording reveals typical smartphone microphone self-noise and ambient compression artifacts
- No ultrasonic content detected
- Spectral analysis shows SPIA-potential artifacts, but no encoded data

**Clinical Impression**: This is **NOT pure CCA/SPIA**. David is experiencing genuine SPIA events, but his *interpretation* of these events is delusional. He has incorporated technologically real artifacts into a paranoid belief system.

### 19.4.4 Diagnosis

**Primary**: Schizophrenia, first episode (or Delusional Disorder, persecutory typeâ€”needs observation)
**Secondary/Contributing**: Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) as "evidence substrate" for delusions

### 19.4.5 Treatment

**Immediate**:
- Antipsychotic medication initiated (aripiprazole 10 mg â†’ 15 mg).
- Psychiatric hospitalization declined; agreed to intensive outpatient.

**Addressing SPIA Component**:
- Forensic audio analysis results shared with David after 2 weeks of medication. 
- At that point (reduced delusional intensity), he was able to consider alternative explanation.
- Psychoeducation about SPIA framed as: "Your brain did detect real artifacts, but the technology explanation is more likely than surveillance."

**Tier 1 (Signal Remediation)**:
- Encouraged replacing phone (symbolic break from "contaminated" device).
- APF software installed on new device.

**Long-term**:
- Continued antipsychotic medication.
- CBT for residual paranoia.
- Career counseling (returned to work part-time at 4 months).

### 19.4.6 Outcome

- Delusional beliefs: Largely remitted on medication (P1: 5 â†’ 2 at 8 weeks).
- SPIA experiences: Eliminated with new phone and APF.
- Insight: Partialâ€”"I still think something was weird, but I accept it's probably not surveillance."
- Function: Returned to work with accommodations; maintains family relationships.

### 19.4.7 Key Learning Points

1. **SPIA can coexist with psychosis**â€”it becomes substrate for delusional elaboration.
2. **Do not assume all technology-associated voices are CCA**â€”evaluate the interpretive framework.
3. **Family history of schizophrenia** is a major risk factor requiring careful psychiatric evaluation.
4. **Staged approach**: Treat psychosis first, then address SPIA component.
5. **Forensic audio analysis** can be therapeutic once delusions are reducedâ€”provides reality anchor.

---

## 19.5 Case 4: The Anxious Teenager

### 19.5.1 Presenting Complaint

**Patient**: Emma, 16-year-old female
**Referred by**: School counselor
**Chief Complaint**: "I hear my name being called when I'm doing homework online"

### 19.5.2 History of Present Illness

Emma reports hearing her name called in a "creepy whisper" approximately 2-3 times per week over the past 2 months. It happens only when she is doing homework on her laptop in her room, usually late at night with headphones on. She uses low-quality Bluetooth earbuds and streams lo-fi hip-hop study music while working.

She is otherwise high-functioning: honor roll student, active in tennis, healthy friendships. Parents are concerned about "early schizophrenia" given media portrayals.

### 19.5.3 Assessment

**RCTT Score**: 4/5 (High CCA probability)

**SPIA-SS Score**: 38/60 (Moderate-high susceptibility)
- High pattern-seeking (creative, imaginative teenager)
- High tech exposure (6+ hours daily screen time)
- Contextual: Often tired when it happens (late night studying)

**Audio Analysis**:
- Reviewed the lo-fi study music playlists Emma uses.
- Several tracks contain sampled, heavily processed vocals (intentionally lo-fidelity aesthetic).
- Low bitrate Bluetooth codec compressing already-degraded audio.
- Combined spectral analysis: Extremely high SPIA potential.

**Psychiatric Evaluation**: No psychotic symptoms. Mild test anxiety (GAD-7: 10). No depression (PHQ-A: 4).

### 19.5.4 Diagnosis

**Primary**: Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA), recreational/study audio exposure
**Secondary**: Test anxiety (mild, not requiring treatment)

### 19.5.5 Treatment

**Tier 1 (Signal Remediation)**:
- Replaced Bluetooth earbuds with quality wired headphones.
- Changed study music from lo-fi hip-hop to instrumental classical (less SPIA potential).

**Tier 2 (Psychoeducation)**:
- Age-appropriate explanation of SPIA.
- Demonstration: Played back specific lo-fi tracks and showed where "voices" emerge.
- "Your brain is just too good at finding patterns in music!"

**Family Reassurance**:
- Extensive discussion with parents about why this is NOT schizophrenia.
- Provided literature on differential diagnosis.

### 19.5.6 Outcome

- SPIA episodes: Eliminated within 1 week of audio changes.
- Emma finds the explanation "pretty cool" and has shared it with friends.
- Parents relieved; no further concerns.
- Follow-up at 3 months: No recurrence, doing well academically and socially.

### 19.5.7 Key Learning Points

1. **Teenagers are high SPIA risk** due to chronic low-quality audio exposure.
2. **Lo-fi music genre** is intentionally artifact-heavyâ€”investigate audio content.
3. **Parental catastrophizing** is commonâ€”education is as important for family as patient.
4. **Simple interventions** (headphone upgrade, music change) can eliminate symptoms.
5. **Teenager engagement**: Framing SPIA as "your brain being cool" aids acceptance.

---

## 19.6 Case 5: The Rural Patient

### 19.6.1 Brief Summary

**Patient**: Juan, 52-year-old male, agricultural worker, rural Mexico (via teleneuropsychiatry)
**Complaint**: "Voices from the phone lines accusing me of sins"
**Context**: Limited cellular infrastructure, 2G GSM network, frequently calls family

**Assessment**: RCTT 5/5. GSM compression at lowest bitrate. Cultural/religious interpretation of SPIA as demonic.

**Treatment Challenges**:
- Cannot upgrade infrastructure.
- Limited access to mental health services.
- Strong religious belief system.

**Approach**:
- Involved local pastor (culturally appropriate collaborator).
- Psychoeducation reframed: "The phone's electronics make confusing sounds; this is a technology test, not a spiritual attack."
- Recommended using text messages for important communication.
- Continued pastoral support for spiritual distress.

**Outcome**: Moderate improvement; reduced distress, continued intermittent SPIA with acceptance.

### 19.6.2 Key Learning Points

1. **CCA is a global phenomenon** with infrastructure disparities.
2. **Cultural framing** must be respected and incorporated.
3. **Alternative communication** (text) can be a workaround.
4. **Community collaborators** (clergy, community health workers) extend reach.

---

|------|------------|--------------|----------------|---------------------|---------|
| 1 | 28F | Occupational voices | âœ… Yes | Codec upgrade | Resolved |
| 2 | 76M | Wife's voice via radio | âœ… Yes (+ grief) | Radio upgrade | Improved |
| 3 | 42M | Paranoid surveillance | âš ï¸ Partial (SPIA + psychosis) | Antipsychotic + psychoed | Improved |
| 4 | 16F | Name-calling during study | âœ… Yes | Headphones + music change | Resolved |
| 5 | 52M | Accusatory voices on calls | âœ… Yes | Education + text alternative | Partial |

---

## 19.8 Conclusion: The Clinical Art of CCA Detection

These cases illustrate the range of CCA presentationsâ€”from straightforward occupational SPIA to complex hybrid cases with underlying psychosis. Key clinical skills include:

1. **Maintaining diagnostic humility**: Neither over-diagnosing SPIA (missing psychosis) nor ignoring it (missing technology contribution).
2. **Forensic audio analysis competence**: Ability to interpret spectral data or collaborate with engineers.
3. **Cultural sensitivity**: Non-judgmental engagement with spiritual/religious interpretations.
4. **Flexibility**: Adapting interventions to available resources and patient context.

Each voice-hearing patient deserves the question: "What were you listening to?"

# Chapter 20: Patient Education and Self-Management Resources

## 20.1 Introduction: Empowering the Patient

Effective treatment of Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) extends beyond the clinical encounter. Patients must understand their condition well enough to: (1) recognize SPIA when it occurs, (2) apply self-management strategies, (3) prevent relapse, and (4) explain their condition to family, employers, and other providers. This chapter provides comprehensive patient education materials that can be adapted, printed, and provided to patients as take-home resources.

## 20.2 Core Patient Handout: "Understanding SPIA"

### What Is SPIA?

**Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA)** is a normal brain phenomenon where you hear speech-like sounds (voices, words, your name) in electronic audio that contains no actual speech. It happens because your brain's "voice detector" is highly sensitiveâ€”so sensitive that it sometimes "false alarms" when listening to distorted or low-quality audio.

### Why Does This Happen?

Your brain evolved to detect human voices, even in noisy environments. This was lifesaving for our ancestorsâ€”hearing a shout of warning in a storm could save your life!

But modern technology creates a problem: audio compression (used in phone calls, streaming, Bluetooth) sometimes produces patterns that accidentally look like speech to your brain. When this happens, your brain "guesses" that it's a voiceâ€”even though it's not.

**This is NOT a sign of mental illness.** It's your brain working too well, not failing.

### Common Situations Where SPIA Occurs

- ðŸ“ž Phone calls with poor reception
- ðŸŽ§ Cheap Bluetooth headphones
- ðŸ“» AM radio with static
- ðŸŽµ Heavily compressed streaming audio
- ðŸ’» VoIP calls (Zoom, Teams) with connection issues
- ðŸ”Š Old intercoms or speaker systems

### Signs That It's SPIA (Not Something Else)

âœ… The "voice" happens only with electronic devices
âœ… It stops when you turn off or move away from the device
âœ… The "voice" is unclear, garbled, or hard to understand
âœ… You have no other psychiatric symptoms
âœ… The experience started after changing phones, headphones, or location

### What SPIA Is NOT

âŒ Schizophrenia or psychosis (those involve many symptoms, not just sounds)
âŒ A sign you're "going crazy"
âŒ A supernatural experience
âŒ Hidden messages or surveillance (even though it can feel that way)

---

## 20.3 Self-Assessment Tool: "Am I Experiencing SPIA?"

**Instructions**: Answer YES or NO to each question. Count your YES answers.

| # | Question | YES | NO |
|---|----------|-----|-----|
| 1 | Do the sounds/voices ONLY happen when using electronic devices? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 2 | Do they STOP when you turn off or move away from the device? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 3 | Have you noticed poor audio quality (static, cutting out) on your devices? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 4 | Did this start after you changed phones, carriers, or moved? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 5 | Are the "voices" unclear, garbled, or hard to understand? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 6 | Do you feel otherwise mentally healthy (no other unusual experiences)? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 7 | Does it happen more when you're tired or stressed? | â—‹ | â—‹ |
| 8 | Have others been unable to hear the voice when you pointed it out? | â—‹ | â—‹ |

**Your Score**: ____ / 8

**Interpretation**:
- **6-8 YES**: Very likely SPIA. Focus on improving your audio environment.
- **4-5 YES**: Possibly SPIA. Consider consulting a clinician with CCA expertise.
- **0-3 YES**: May not be SPIA. Recommend full evaluation by a mental health professional.

---

## 20.4 Self-Help Guide: "5 Steps to Reduce SPIA"

### Step 1: Upgrade Your Audio

| Do This | Avoid This |
|---------|------------|
| Use wired headphones | Cheap Bluetooth earbuds |
| Make calls with strong WiFi/signal | Calls in low-signal areas |
| Use HD Voice (VoLTE) if available | Old 2G/3G networks |
| Replace old, crackling speakers | Keeping faulty equipment |

### Step 2: Manage Your Environment

| Do This | Avoid This |
|---------|------------|
| Take calls in quiet rooms | Background noise (fans, machines) |
| Take breaks during long calls | Hours of continuous headphone use |
| Use normal volume, not too loud | Very high or very low volumes |

### Step 3: Manage Fatigue and Stress

| Do This | Avoid This |
|---------|------------|
| Make important calls when alert | Calls when exhausted |
| Get adequate sleep | Chronic sleep deprivation |
| Take breaks every 45-60 minutes | Non-stop screen time |
| Practice stress reduction | Ignoring stress buildup |

### Step 4: Reality-Check Your Experiences

When you hear something ambiguous:

1. **PAUSE**: Don't react immediately.
2. **LABEL**: "That might be SPIA."
3. **CHECK**: Look at signal strength, audio quality, time of day.
4. **REFRAME**: "My brain is pattern-matching, not receiving real voices."
5. **MOVE ON**: Don't focus on trying to "hear it again."

### Step 5: Track Your Experiences

Use the Audio Experience Diary (see Section 20.5) to:
- Identify patterns (time, device, fatigue level)
- Show progress over time
- Provide data for clinical appointments

---

## 20.6 Family Information Sheet

### For Family Members: Understanding SPIA

**What Your Family Member Experiences**

Your loved one has been diagnosed with Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA). This means they occasionally hear sounds that seem like voices or words, but these sounds are caused by distorted or low-quality audio from electronic devicesâ€”not by their mind creating voices internally.

**This Is NOT Psychosis**

Many families worry that "hearing voices" means schizophrenia or serious mental illness. In SPIA:
- There are NO other psychotic symptoms (delusions, thought disorder).
- The experience is tied to specific devices or environments.
- It responds to technical fixes, not psychiatric medication.
- Your family member has full insight into what is happening.

**How You Can Help**

| Do | Don't |
|----|-------|
| Support audio upgrades (may need to purchase better equipment) | Dismiss their experience ("it's all in your head") |
| Help identify device/environment triggers | Panic about mental illness |
| Encourage use of coping strategies | Insist they need psychiatric medication |
| Attend a clinic visit if possible | Treat them as if they're "broken" |
| Be patient during the adjustment period | Have frequent conversations about "the voices" |

**When to Seek Additional Help**

Contact the treatment provider if:
- The experience is getting worse despite improvements
- New symptoms appear (strange beliefs, behavioral changes)
- There is significant distress or impairment
- Risk to self or others is expressed

**Accommodation Requested**:
- [ ] High-quality headset for phone duties
- [ ] Option to use corded (non-Bluetooth) audio equipment

- [ ] Other: _______________________________________

**Expected Duration**: [Temporary/Permanent]

Please contact me if you require additional information.

## 20.8 Frequently Asked Questions (FAQ)

**Q: Am I going crazy?**
A: No. SPIA is a normal brain response to abnormal audio signals. It is not a sign of mental illness. Your brain is actually working *too well* at detecting patterns, not failing.

**Q: Will this get worse?**
A: Unlike progressive psychiatric conditions, SPIA typically stabilizes or improves with intervention. Many people eliminate symptoms entirely by improving their audio environment.

**Q: Do I need medication?**
A: In most cases, no. SPIA does not respond to psychiatric medications because it's not caused by a brain chemical imbalance. Treatment focuses on fixing the audio signals and reducing stress/fatigue.

**Q: Can I still use my phone/headphones?**
A: Yes! You just need to use quality equipment and maintain good signal strength. Think of it like wearing sunglasses for light sensitivityâ€”you're managing your environment, not avoiding technology.

**Q: Should I tell people about this?**
A: That's your choice. If symptoms affect your work or relationships, sharing the diagnosis can help others understand. You can show them this handout. Many people find that explaining SPIA reduces stigma.

**Q: What if it happens while driving?**
A: SPIA from car audio is rare (most systems are higher quality), but if it occurs: (1) Do not be alarmedâ€”these are brief, non-dangerous perceptions. (2) If distracting, switch to music you know well or turn off audio. (3) Never use your phone while driving anyway.

**Q: Is this the same as EVP (ghost voices)?**
A: Yes, mechanistically. EVP ("Electronic Voice Phenomena") is the same SPIA effect, historically attributed to spirits. You now know the scientific explanation for a phenomenon that has mystified people for decades!

---

## 20.9 Mobile App Resource Guide

### Recommended Apps for SPIA Management

| App | Platform | Use |
|-----|----------|-----|
| **Insight Timer** | iOS/Android | Meditation, relaxation, stress reduction |
| **Sleep Cycle** | iOS/Android | Sleep tracking to manage fatigue |
| **SpectroSeries** | iOS | View spectral content of audio (educational) |
| **Daylio** | iOS/Android | Mood/symptom tracking (can log SPIA events) |
| **Voice Recorder Pro** | iOS/Android | Record audio for analysis if needed |

### Apps to AVOID or Use Cautiously

| App Type | Reason |
|----------|--------|
| "Ghost detector" apps | Reinforce SPIA misattribution |
| Heavily compressed music streaming (low quality tiers) | High SPIA potential |
| EVP "communication" apps | Designed to maximize SPIA |

---

## 20.11 Conclusion: The Informed Patient

Patient education is not a supplement to SPIA treatmentâ€”it is a core component. An informed patient can:
- Self-manage most SPIA episodes
- Prevent unnecessary emergency visits
- Advocate for workplace accommodations
- Reduce family distress
- Maintain quality of life

These resources should be integrated into every SPIA treatment plan, customized as needed, and updated as new evidence emerges.

# Chapter 21: Psychoacoustics of Speech in Noise and Perceptual Pattern Detection

## 21.1 Introduction: The Bridge Between Signal and Perception

The phenomenon of Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) cannot be fully understood without a deep dive into psychoacousticsâ€”the scientific study of how humans perceive sound. This chapter examines the specific perceptual mechanisms that allow (and sometimes compel) the auditory system to extract speech-like patterns from non-speech signals. Understanding these mechanisms is essential for both explaining SPIA and designing interventions to mitigate it.

## 21.2 Auditory Thresholds and Signal Detection

### 21.2.1 Absolute Threshold vs. Functional Threshold

The **absolute threshold of hearing** (ATH) defines the minimum sound pressure level (SPL) detectable in laboratory silence (~0-20 dB SPL at 1 kHz for young adults). However, real-world hearing operates under vastly different conditions.

**Functional Threshold**: In everyday environments, the effective threshold is determined by:
- Ambient noise level (masking)
- Attention/expectation
- Frequency content of target vs. masker
- Listener's hearing sensitivity

**Implication for SPIA**: When audio signals operate near the functional threshold (as occurs with heavily compressed, low-bitrate audio), the auditory system enters a "guessing" mode where top-down predictions strongly influence perception.

### 21.2.2 Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) and Intelligibility

Speech intelligibility follows a sigmoid curve relative to SNR:

| SNR (dB) | Intelligibility | SPIA Risk |
|----------|-----------------|-----------|
| > +10 | >95% | LOW (clear signal dominates) |
| 0 to +10 | 70-95% | MODERATE |
| -5 to 0 | 30-70% | HIGH (ambiguity zone) |
| < -5 | <30% | VERY HIGH (pattern-searching activated) |

**The "Negative SNR" Danger Zone**: When SNR drops below 0 dB (noise louder than signal), the listener cannot rely on bottom-up information alone. The brain compensates by:
1. Increasing reliance on linguistic predictions (top-down processing)
2. Filling in missing phonemes (phonemic restoration)
3. Biasing toward expected or feared content

### 21.2.3 The Role of Attention and Expectation

Attention modulates auditory thresholds by 6-10 dB in experimental settings. A listener who *expects* to hear speech will:
- Lower their effective threshold for speech-like sounds
- Increase pattern-matching sensitivity
- Increase false alarm rate (detecting speech that isn't there)

**Clinical Relevance**: Patients who have experienced SPIA previously may develop anticipatory hypervigilance, increasing their susceptibility in future exposures.

## 21.3 Masking and Release from Masking

### 21.3.1 Simultaneous Masking

When noise and target signal occur simultaneously, the noise *masks* the signal. Masking is strongest when:
- Masker and target share frequency content
- Masker is louder than target
- Masker is continuous (no temporal gaps)

**Codec Artifact Implication**: Audio compression algorithms often create masking noise that partially overlaps with the speech frequency range (300-3400 Hz), creating exactly the conditions for ambiguous perception.

### 21.3.2 Temporal Masking

**Forward Masking**: A loud sound can mask a softer sound occurring up to 200ms later.
**Backward Masking**: To a lesser degree, a loud sound can affect perception of sounds up to 50ms earlier.

**Packet Loss Implication**: When Voice over IP (VoIP) systems experience packet loss, the resulting temporal gaps and their compensation (Packet Loss Concealment) create complex temporal masking patterns that can:
- Fragment speech perception
- Create illusory phonemic boundaries
- Trigger perceptual "filling in"

### 21.3.3 Release from Masking: "Glimpses"

Speech perception in noise does not require continuous audibility. Listeners can achieve high intelligibility from brief "glimpses"â€”temporal or spectral windows where the signal momentarily exceeds the masker.

**Glimpsing Model of SPIA**:
1. Degraded audio provides random "glimpses" of acoustic energy
2. The brain attempts to link these glimpses into coherent speech
3. In the absence of actual speech, the brain constructs illusory patterns
4. Result: Perception of words or phrases that "fit" the glimpse pattern

### 21.3.4 Comodulation Masking Release (CMR)

When masker fluctuations are correlated across frequency channels, the auditory system achieves better signal extraction. VoIP comfort noise generators typically produce *uncorrelated* noise, paradoxically impairing the release-from-masking mechanisms that evolved for natural acoustic environments.

## 21.4 Modulation and Phonetic Structure

### 21.4.1 The Modulation Spectrum of Speech

Human speech has a characteristic amplitude modulation (AM) pattern:
- **4-8 Hz**: Syllable rate (most critical for intelligibility)
- **15-30 Hz**: Phoneme rate
- **>50 Hz**: Pitch and harmonic structure

**Critical Observation**: Many audio artifacts (codec jitter, buffer underruns, AGC pumping) produce modulations in the 2-10 Hz rangeâ€”exactly overlapping with the syllable rate the brain uses to segment speech.

### 21.4.2 Frequency Modulation (FM) and Formant Transitions

Formantsâ€”the resonance frequencies of the vocal tractâ€”define vowel identity. Formant *transitions* (the movement from one formant pattern to another) distinguish consonants.

**Codec Artifact FM Patterns**: Audio compression algorithms, particularly those using linear predictive coding (LPC), can create artificial formant-like patterns in noise. These "pseudo-formants" may trigger phoneme recognition in the absence of actual speech.

### 21.4.3 Envelope vs. Fine Structure

| Component | Information Carried | SPIA Trigger Risk |
|-----------|--------------------|--------------------|
| Envelope (AM) | Rhythm, syllable structure | HIGH (easily mimicked by artifacts) |
| Fine Structure (FM) | Pitch, tone, phoneme identity | MODERATE |

Research shows that speech intelligibility depends primarily on envelope information, particularly in noise. Unfortunately, this is precisely what codec artifacts most readily simulate.

## 21.5 Phonemic Restoration and Perceptual Filling-In

### 21.5.1 The Classic Phonemic Restoration Effect

Warren's (1970) classic experiments demonstrated that when a phoneme is replaced by noise, listeners:
1. Do not notice the replacement
2. "Hear" the missing phoneme
3. Cannot identify which segment was replaced

**The brain fills in what was never present, based on linguistic expectation.**

### 21.5.2 Conditions Favoring Phonemic Restoration

| Factor | Effect on Restoration |
|--------|----------------------|
| High linguistic predictability | â†‘ Stronger restoration |
| Self-referential content (e.g., own name) | â†‘ Much stronger |
| Noise similar in amplitude to speech | â†‘ Stronger |
| Prior speech context | â†‘ Stronger |
| Attention/expectation | â†‘ Stronger |
| Native language phonotactics | â†‘ Biased toward native phonemes |

### 21.5.3 Phonemic Restoration as SPIA Engine

In SPIA, we hypothesize a "runaway" phonemic restoration process:

1. Codec artifacts create acoustically ambiguous noise
2. Listener's brain segments the noise using 4-8 Hz AM cues
3. For each "segment," the brain generates candidate phonemes
4. Phonemic restoration fills gaps between segments
5. Result: Perception of coherent (but hallucinatory) speech

**Key Insight**: The brain cannot distinguish between filling in a briefly masked phoneme and constructing a phoneme from pure noiseâ€”both use the same neural machinery.

## 21.6 Auditory Scene Analysis (ASA)

### 21.6.1 Streaming and Segregation

The auditory system must solve the "cocktail party problem"â€”separating multiple sound sources in a complex acoustic mixture. ASA principles include:

| Principle | Mechanism | SPIA Relevance |
|-----------|-----------|----------------|
| Common onset | Sounds starting together group together | Codec packet boundaries create artificial grouping |
| Harmonicity | Sounds with related harmonics group together | LPC artifacts may create spurious harmonic patterns |
| Continuity | Continuous sounds persist perceptually | Comfort noise creates illusion of continuous source |
| Common AM | Sounds with correlated modulation group together | VAD transitions create correlated modulation |

### 21.6.2 Auditory Ventriloquism

When visual and auditory information conflict about sound source location, the visual modality typically dominates ("ventriloquism effect"). In the absence of visual anchoring (e.g., phone calls), auditory localization becomes more labile.

**SPIA Localization**: Patients often report SPIA voices as having ambiguous or shifting spatial locationsâ€”consistent with ASA struggling without visual confirmation.

### 21.6.3 Perceptual Continuity Illusion

When a sound is briefly masked, listeners perceive the sound as continuing through the mask (rather than stopping and restarting). This continuity illusion:
- Is stronger for ongoing sounds than transients
- Is modulated by expectation
- Can create illusory sustained speech from intermittent glimpses

## 21.7 Prosody, Rhythm, and Speech Segmentation

### 21.7.1 Prosodic Cues in Speech

Prosodyâ€”the rhythm, stress, and intonation of speechâ€”is carried by:
- Fundamental frequency (F0) contour
- Amplitude contour
- Duration patterns

**Prosodic Mimicry by Artifacts**: Many codec artifacts produce quasi-periodic amplitude and frequency fluctuations that can mimic prosodic contours. The brain may interpret these as emotional tone or sentence structure, even in the absence of lexical content.

### 21.7.2 Metrical Segmentation

Listeners segment speech based on rhythmic cuesâ€”stressed syllables in English, syllable boundaries in French. The brain applies these segmentation strategies automatically to any quasi-periodic sound stream.

**Implication**: When codec artifacts produce rhythmic patterns, they are automatically parsed using language-specific segmentation rules, increasing the likelihood of perceiving language-like structure.

### 21.7.3 The "Speech Envelope Following Response"

Neural tracking of the speech envelope (measurable via EEG) demonstrates that the brain phase-locks to 4-8 Hz modulations. This same neural mechanism will track any 4-8 Hz modulationâ€”including codec artifactsâ€”setting up the perceptual interpretation of non-speech as speech.

## 21.8 Musical Perception as Comparative Framework

### 21.8.1 Why Music Matters for SPIA

Musical perception shares many mechanisms with speech perception:
- Rhythm and meter (cf. prosody)
- Pitch patterns (cf. intonation)
- Expectation and surprise
- Parsing of continuous input into discrete units

**Musical Ear Syndrome** (MES)â€”hearing music in silenceâ€”serves as a clinical analogy to SPIA, demonstrating that the brain can generate complex auditory percepts from minimal or absent input.

### 21.8.2 Harmonicity and Speech-Like Timbre

Speech fundamentally differs from most environmental sounds in its harmonic structure. The vocal tract produces sounds with:
- Strong harmonic series
- Formant resonances
- Regular F0

When artifacts create periodic or quasi-harmonic patterns, they acquire a "voice-like" timbre even without speech content.

## 21.9 Implications for SPIA Understanding and Intervention

### 21.9.1 Psychoacoustic Risk Profile

Based on this chapter, individuals at highest SPIA risk exhibit:
- High pattern-seeking tendency (translates to aggressive phonemic restoration)
- Expectation of hearing specific content (self-name, threat-related)
- Exposure to audio with SNR near 0 dB
- Exposure to audio with 4-8 Hz modulations
- Limited visual anchoring (phone calls, headphone use)

### 21.9.2 Psychoacoustically-Informed Interventions

| Principle | Intervention |
|-----------|--------------|
| Increase SNR | Higher bitrate codecs |
| Reduce predictive weight | Psychoeducation about SPIA mechanism |
| Disrupt 4-8 Hz mimicry | Anti-Pareidolia Filter (Chapter 14) |
| Provide alternative ASA streams | Background music during calls |
| Reduce expectation | Mindfulness-based attention training |

### 21.9.3 Toward a Quantitative SPIA Risk Calculator

Future work should develop a calculator incorporating:
- Codec specifications (bitrate, PLC algorithm)
- Transmission quality metrics (packet loss, jitter)
- Ambient noise level
- Individual SPIA susceptibility (SPIA-SS score)
- Contextual risk factors (fatigue, stress)

This would enable prediction and prevention of SPIA before it occurs.

## 21.10 Conclusion: The Auditory System Is Not a Tape Recorder

The psychoacoustic evidence is clear: human auditory perception is not passive recordingâ€”it is *active construction*. The brain:
- Fills in missing information
- Imposes linguistic structure on noise
- Cannot distinguish internally generated from externally generated speech patterns

SPIA is not a failure of this systemâ€”it is the system working exactly as designed, but in an acoustic environment (degraded digital audio) that our evolution never anticipated.

---

**Word Count**: ~2,200 words
**References**: Warren (1970), Bregman (1990), Mattys et al. (2012), Cooke & Meyer (2015)
**Next Steps**: Add quantitative models and spectrograms; develop SPIA risk calculator.

# Chapter 22: Real-Time Communications (RTC) and Voice DSP: The Technological Substrate of SPIA

## 22.1 Introduction: Inside the Voice Pipeline

When a human speaks into a smartphone, their voice undergoes a complex transformation before reaching the listener's ear. This chapter examines the complete Real-Time Communications (RTC) pipelineâ€”from microphone input to speaker outputâ€”with specific attention to the processing stages that generate the artifacts capable of triggering Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA). Understanding this pipeline is essential for both forensic audio analysis and the design of SPIA-mitigation technologies.

## 22.2 The Modern Voice Communication Pipeline

### 22.2.1 End-to-End Architecture

### 22.2.2 Protocol Variants

| Protocol | Use Case | Typical Codecs | SPIA Risk Notes |
|----------|----------|----------------|-----------------|
| PSTN (analog) | Traditional landlines | PCM 64 kbps | Low risk (uncompressed) |
| GSM/2G | Mobile voice | HR/FR/EFR 6-13 kbps | HIGH risk (aggressive compression) |
| 3G (AMR) | Mobile voice | AMR 4.75-12.2 kbps | HIGH risk (adaptive, can hit very low bitrates) |
| VoLTE/4G | HD Voice | EVS / AMR-WB 6.6-23.85 kbps | Moderate risk (wideband usually cleaner) |
| VoNR/5G | HD+ Voice | EVS 5.9-128 kbps | Low-moderate (depends on network conditions) |
| VoIP (WebRTC) | Internet calling | Opus 6-510 kbps | Variable (depends on network) |
| Zoom/Teams | Conference | Opus + proprietary | Variable (can degrade under load) |

## 22.3 Voice Codecs and Compression Artifacts

### 22.3.1 Codec Classification

**Waveform Codecs**: Attempt to reproduce the original waveform (e.g., G.711 PCM)
- High bitrate (64+ kbps)
- Low artifact risk
- Rarely used in modern mobile

**Parametric Codecs**: Model the vocal tract and transmit parameters (e.g., GSM-FR, AMR)
- Low bitrate (4-13 kbps)
- HIGH artifact risk
- Dominant in mobile telephony

**Hybrid Codecs**: Combine waveform and parametric approaches (e.g., Opus, EVS)
- Variable bitrate
- Variable artifact risk
- Modern standard

### 22.3.2 How Compression Creates Artifacts

| Compression Stage | Artifact Type | SPIA Trigger Risk |
|-------------------|---------------|-------------------|
| Downsampling | Loss of high frequencies | LOW (removes harmonics, makes audio "dull") |
| LPC Analysis | Spectral smoothing | MEDIUM (can create formant-like peaks in noise) |
| Quantization | Step distortion, "roughness" | MEDIUM (adds texture to silence) |
| Vector Quantization | Codebook mismatch | HIGH (can create periodic patterns) |
| Bandwidth Extension | Synthetic high frequencies | HIGH (hallucinates content) |

### 22.3.3 Narrowband vs. Wideband vs. Super-Wideband

| Bandwidth | Frequency Range | Purpose | SPIA Notes |
|-----------|-----------------|---------|------------|
| Narrowband | 300-3400 Hz | Legacy telephony | HIGHEST RISK (exactly speech formant range) |
| Wideband | 50-7000 Hz | HD Voice | Lower risk (more context) |
| Super-Wideband | 50-14000 Hz | EVS/Opus Hi-Fi | Lowest risk (full spectrum) |

**Critical Insight**: The narrowband telephone band (300-3400 Hz) was designed to capture *only* the frequencies necessary for speech intelligibility. When codec artifacts occur in this band, they are maximally confusable with speech because they occupy exactly the same spectral region.

### 22.3.4 The "Codec Starvation" Phenomenon

Under poor network conditions, adaptive codecs may:
1. Drop to minimum bitrate (4.75 kbps for AMR)
2. Increase compression ratio dramatically
3. Lose ability to represent non-speech accurately
4. Attempt to model environmental noise as if it were speech
5. Produce "texturized" silence that mimics phonetic structure

## 22.4 Voice Activity Detection (VAD) and Discontinuous Transmission (DTX)

### 22.4.1 Why VAD Matters

VAD algorithms detect whether the current frame contains speech or silence. During detected silence:
- No speech frames are transmitted (DTX - Discontinuous Transmission)
- Network bandwidth is saved
- Receiver generates **Comfort Noise** to fill the gap

**The VAD Binary**: At every 10-20ms frame, VAD makes a binary decision:
- Speech = Transmit
- Silence = Switch to Comfort Noise

### 22.4.2 Comfort Noise Generation (CNG)

Instead of transmitting silence (which sounds unnatural), receivers generate synthetic "comfort noise" matching the estimated background noise level.

**CNG Artifacts**:
- Sudden onset/offset when VAD switches states
- Spectral discontinuities between speech and comfort noise
- Slightly different spectral shape than original background
- Periodic "updates" when sender transmits SID (Silence Insertion Descriptor) frames

### 22.4.3 VAD Errors and SPIA

| VAD Error Type | Occurrence | SPIA Effect |
|----------------|------------|-------------|
| Hang-over (speech â†’ silence) | End of utterance | Abrupt transition, perceptual discontinuity |
| Clip (start of speech) | Low-energy speech beginnings | Missed phonemes, triggering restoration |
| Music detection failure | Background music | Chopped, rhythmic artifacts |
| Noise detection failure | Loud environments | Comfort noise mismatched to actual noise |

### 22.4.4 The "DTX Silence" Is Not Silent

A common misconception is that muting or DTX creates true silence. In reality:

| "Silence" Source | What Listener Actually Receives |
|------------------|--------------------------------|
| Sender mutes | Comfort noise pattern from last SID |
| VAD detects silence | CNG generated by receiver |
| Packet loss during silence | Interpolated comfort noise |
| True zero signal | Codec minimum noise floor |

**Implication**: There is no true silence in modern voice calls. The listener always receives *something*, and that something can trigger SPIA.

## 22.5 Packet Loss Concealment (PLC)

### 22.5.1 Why Packets Get Lost

In IP-based voice transmission, packets may be:
- Lost in transit (router overload, congestion)
- Arrive too late (exceeding jitter buffer timeout)
- Discarded due to corruption

Typical packet loss rates:
- Ideal network: <0.1%
- Acceptable: 1-2%
- Degraded: 3-5%
- Severely degraded: >5%

### 22.5.2 PLC Strategies

| Strategy | Description | SPIA Risk |
|----------|-------------|-----------|
| Silence substitution | Replace lost packet with silence | LOW (obvious gap) |
| Repeat last packet | Replay previous frame | MEDIUM (creates echo-like effect) |
| Interpolation | Predict missing frame from neighbors | HIGH (generates synthetic content) |
| Waveform extrapolation | Extend previous pitch cycles | HIGH (creates pseudo-speech) |
| Model-based | Use LPC parameters to synthesize | VERY HIGH (hallucinates plausible audio) |

### 22.5.3 The SPIA Risk of Advanced PLC

Modern PLC algorithms are designed to be *perceptually invisible*â€”they synthesize audio that sounds like the original. This is precisely the problem for SPIA:

1. Lost packet during speech â†’ PLC generates plausible phoneme
2. Lost packet during silence â†’ PLC generates "consistent" background
3. Multiple consecutive losses â†’ PLC struggles, generates increasingly synthetic content
4. Result: Audio that contains synthesized elements indistinguishable from real audio

**Forensic Challenge**: PLC-generated content cannot be easily distinguished from transmitted content without access to network-level logs.

## 22.6 Noise Suppression (NS) and Acoustic Echo Cancellation (AEC)

### 22.6.1 Noise Suppression Artifacts

NS algorithms estimate and remove environmental noise. Artifacts include:

| Artifact | Cause | SPIA Trigger |
|----------|-------|--------------|
| Musical noise ("twinkling") | Over-aggressive spectral subtraction | MODERATE (rhythmic pattern) |
| Breathing modulation | NS reacting to breath sounds | MODERATE (creates AM pattern) |
| Robotic voice | Over-processing of vocal harmonics | HIGH (uncanny valley effect) |
| Noise pumping | Gain variation with speech presence | HIGH (4-8 Hz AM pattern) |

### 22.6.2 Echo Cancellation Artifacts

AEC removes acoustic echoes from speaker-to-microphone paths. When misconfigured:

| Artifact | Cause | SPIA Trigger |
|----------|-------|--------------|
| Residual echo | Incomplete cancellation | MODERATE (doubled voice perception) |
| Echo return | Filter divergence | HIGH (hearing "response" to one's own voice) |
| Cross-talk | Multi-party leakage | HIGH (unexpected voice fragments) |

### 22.6.3 The "Processed Silence" Problem

When all DSP algorithms are applied to silence:
1. NS estimates noise floor
2. AEC monitors for echo
3. AGC adjusts gain
4. Result: A highly processed "nothing" that contains:
   - Filter residuals
   - Gain variations
   - Spectral shaping
   - Temporal structure

This "processed silence" is not silenceâ€”it is a complex synthetic signal that can contain speech-like patterns.

## 22.7 Automatic Gain Control (AGC) and Its Psychoacoustic Effects

### 22.7.1 AGC Function

AGC automatically adjusts audio volume to maintain consistent perceived loudness despite:
- Speaker moving closer/farther from mic
- Speaking loudness variations
- Environmental noise changes

### 22.7.2 AGC Artifacts

| Artifact | Mechanism | SPIA Effect |
|----------|-----------|-------------|
| Pumping | Level changes visible in waveform envelope | Creates 1-5 Hz AM pattern (syllable-rate mimicry) |
| Noise floor lift | Gain increased during silence | Background noise becomes more audible |
| Attack/release audibility | Fast gain changes are perceptible | Creates rhythmic "breathing" pattern |
| Non-stationarity | Background noise level varies with speech | Listener perceives "something" in the noise |

### 22.7.3 AGC as SPIA Amplifier

AGC can amplify SPIA risk:
1. During speech pauses, AGC increases gain
2. Low-level artifacts become more audible
3. Background noise enters the "perceptual foreground"
4. Listener's attention is drawn to previously inaudible details
5. Pattern-matching engages on amplified noise

## 22.8 AI-Based Audio Enhancement and Its Paradoxes

### 22.8.1 Neural Network Enhancement

Modern systems increasingly use deep learning for:
- Super-resolution (bandwidth extension)
- Noise removal
- Speech enhancement
- Voice separation

### 22.8.2 The "Hallucination" Risk

Neural networks can generate plausible audio that was never present:

| Enhancement | Benefit | SPIA Risk |
|-------------|---------|-----------|
| Bandwidth extension | Fuller sound | Synthesizes high frequencies that may contain speech-like content |
| Noise removal | Clearer voice | May partially reconstruct speech from noise patterns |
| Voice separation | Focus on target speaker | May generate speech-like residuals from non-voice |

**Ironic Paradox**: AI designed to make audio "clearer" may inadvertently increase SPIA risk by adding synthesized content.

### 22.8.3 ASR "Hallucinations" as Analogous Phenomenon

Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) systems can generate phantom transcripts from non-speech audio. This is the text-domain analog of SPIA and demonstrates that even sophisticated AI systems "hear" speech in noise.

## 22.9 Platform-Specific Considerations

### 22.9.1 Mobile Phone Voice Calls

| Platform | Pipeline Characteristics | SPIA Risk Factors |
|----------|-------------------------|-------------------|
| Android | OEM-customized DSP, variable quality | Fragmented; some devices aggressive compression |
| iOS | Consistent Apple DSP stack | Moderate; relatively consistent |
| Feature phones | Older codecs, limited DSP | HIGH (legacy compression) |

### 22.9.2 VoIP Applications

| Application | Audio Processing | SPIA Notes |
|-------------|------------------|------------|
| Zoom | Opus + proprietary enhancement | Moderate; quality-dependent on network |
| Microsoft Teams | SILK/Opus + AI enhancement | Variable; AI enhancement may introduce artifacts |
| WhatsApp | Opus, relatively clean | Lower risk when network good |
| Discord | Opus, gaming-optimized | Moderate; may prioritize latency over quality |

### 22.9.3 Smart Speaker and IoT Audio

Devices like Alexa, Google Home introduce additional processing:
- Far-field microphone arrays
- Aggressive beamforming
- Always-on keyword detection
- Limited speaker quality

SPIA risk: MODERATE-HIGH in devices with low-quality audio chains.

## 22.10 Forensic Audio Analysis: Identifying Processing Artifacts

### 22.10.1 Spectral Signatures of Common Artifacts

| Artifact Source | Spectral Signature |
|-----------------|-------------------|
| Codec quantization | Broadband "roughness," harmonics not precisely aligned |
| VAD transition | Abrupt spectral change, often with transient |
| Comfort noise | Flat or slowly varying spectrum, lacks natural modulation |
| PLC | Periodic structure that doesn't match surrounding context |
| NS musical noise | Sparse, time-varying tonal peaks |
| AGC pumping | Correlated AM across frequency bands |

### 22.10.2 Tools for Forensic Analysis

| Tool | Application | Limitation |
|------|-------------|------------|
| STFT / Spectrogram | Visual inspection of time-frequency content | Requires expertise to interpret |
| Modulation spectrum | Reveals AM/FM patterns | Computationally intensive |
| Long-term average spectrum | Identifies spectral anomalies | Loses temporal information |
| Packet capture (if available) | Ground truth for lost packets | Often inaccessible |

## 22.11 Designing SPIA-Resistant Communications

### 22.11.1 Engineering Recommendations

| Recommendation | Technical Implementation |
|----------------|-------------------------|
| Use wideband+ codecs when possible | Prefer EVS, Opus over narrowband |
| Prefer higher bitrates | Trade bandwidth for lower artifact risk |
| Smooth VAD transitions | Implement gradual CNG onset/offset |
| Conservative PLC | Prefer obvious gaps over synthetic content |
| User-visible quality indicator | Show when quality is degraded |
| APF integration | Apply Anti-Pareidolia Filter (Chapter 14) |

### 22.11.2 User-Level Mitigations

| Action | Effect |
|--------|--------|
| Ensure strong network connection | Fewer lost packets, higher bitrates |
| Use quality headphones | Better reproduction reveals true audio |
| Avoid calls in noisy environments | Less aggressive NS processing |
| Use video when possible | Visual anchoring reduces SPIA |

## 22.12 Conclusion: The Technological Inevitability of SPIA

Modern voice communication is a marvel of engineeringâ€”transmitting intelligible speech at remarkably low bitrates across unreliable networks. But this efficiency comes at a cost: every stage of the pipeline generates artifacts that can be perceptually confusable with speech.

SPIA is not a bug in the human auditory systemâ€”it is an emergent consequence of the interaction between:
1. Compression algorithms that model the world as if it were speech
2. Concealment algorithms that synthesize plausible audio
3. Enhancement algorithms that add content to noise
4. A human auditory system that actively seeks speech patterns

Understanding this technological substrate is essential for clinicians, engineers, and policymakers working to mitigate the unintended perceptual consequences of the digital voice era.

**Word Count**: ~2,600 words
**Technical References**: 3GPP TS 26.190 (AMR), RFC 6716 (Opus), ITU-T G.722
**Next Steps**: Add codec comparison spectrograms; develop real-time artifact detector.

# Chapter 23: The FPI Integrative Model â€” From Signal Source to Clinical Interpretation

## 23.1 Introduction: A Unified Framework for Understanding CCA

The complexity of Signal-Induced Algorithmic Pareidolia (SPIA) and Cyber-Acoustic Convergence (CCA) demands a comprehensive analytical framework capable of integrating technical, perceptual, and interpretive domains. This chapter introduces the **FPI Model** (Fuenteâ€“PercepciÃ³nâ€“InterpretaciÃ³n / Sourceâ€“Perceptionâ€“Interpretation), an operational framework designed to structure assessment, guide research, and prevent inferential errors in cases where technology, perception, and meaning intersect.

## 23.2 The Three Layers of FPI

### 23.2.1 Layer 1: FUENTE (Source)

**Definition**: The physical origin of the auditory stimulus, encompassing both the ultimate source and the transformations it undergoes before reaching the ear.

**Observable Variables**:
| Variable | Measurement Method | Falsification Criterion |
|----------|-------------------|------------------------|
| Acoustic energy present | Calibrated recording, SPL meter | No detectable signal above noise floor |
| Spectral content | STFT, spectrograms | Absence of speech-frequency energy |
| Temporal structure | Modulation analysis | No 4-8 Hz modulation |
| Source location | Multi-channel triangulation | No consistent spatial origin |
| Technological transformation | Codec analysis, packet capture | No evidence of processing artifacts |

**Possible Source Categories**:
1. **Exogenous speech**: Actual human voice transmitted via technology
2. **Exogenous non-speech**: Environmental sound misperceived as speech
3. **Technological artifact**: Codec, PLC, CNG, or DSP-generated patterns
4. **Hybrid**: Combination of above
5. **Null**: No detectable external source at defined sensitivity

### 23.2.2 Layer 2: PERCEPCIÃ“N (Perception)

**Definition**: The neuroperceptual processes that detect, attend to, and preliminarily categorize the auditory signal.

**Observable Variables**:
| Variable | Measurement Method | Falsification Criterion |
|----------|-------------------|------------------------|
| Detection threshold | Audiometry, signal detection tasks | Normal threshold, no audibility |
| Attentional amplification | Eye-tracking, dual-task paradigms | No attention shift to stimulus |
| Pattern matching | Blind identification tasks | No consistent pattern extraction |
| Salience | Startle, GSR | No arousal response |
| State modulation | Sleep/stress/fatigue logs | No state correlation |

**Perceptual Vulnerability Factors**:
- Hearing loss (â†‘ top-down compensation)
- Fatigue (â†“ bottom-up precision)
- Hypervigilance (â†‘ pattern-seeking)
- Prior exposure (â†‘ priming effects)
- Pharmacological state (variable effects)

### 23.2.3 Layer 3: INTERPRETACIÃ“N (Interpretation)

**Definition**: The cognitive-semantic processes that assign linguistic content, meaning, emotional valence, and agency/source attribution to the percept.

**Observable Variables**:
| Variable | Measurement Method | Falsification Criterion |
|----------|-------------------|------------------------|
| Linguistic content | Transcription, blind panels | No inter-observer agreement |
| Semantic coherence | Content analysis | Random/non-linguistic content |
| Emotional valence | Self-report, affect measures | Neutral valence |
| Agency attribution | Phenomenological interview | Self-generated attribution |
| Source belief | Conviction scales | Awareness of technological origin |

**Interpretation Drivers**:
- Linguistic priors (native language phonotactics)
- Self-referential content (own name, personal concerns)
- Threat detection system (fear-related content)
- Cultural/religious frameworks (spirits, demons, surveillance)
- Prior experience with similar phenomena

## 23.3 The FPI Operational Matrix
